# Searching Sourcegraph

Search before you build. Existing patterns reduce tokens, ensure consistency, and surface tested solutions.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Tool Selection Logic

**Start here:**

1. **Know the exact symbol or pattern?** → `sg_keyword_search`
2. **Know the concept, not the code?** → `sg_nls_search`
3. **Need to understand how/why?** → `sg_deepsearch_read`
4. **Tracing a symbol's usage?** → `sg_find_references`
5. **Need full implementation?** → `sg_go_to_definition` → `sg_read_file`

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Exact code patterns | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Trace usage | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Understand systems | `sg_deepsearch_read` |
| Read files | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
file:.*\.test\.ts$ -file:__mocks__   # Tests, exclude mocks
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

Combine filters: `repo:^github.com/myorg/backend$ file:src/handlers lang:typescript`

## Context-Aware Behaviour

**When the user provides a file path or error message:**
- Extract symbols, function names, or error codes
- Search for those exact terms first
- Trace references if the error involves a known symbol

**When the user asks "how does X work":**
- Prefer `sg_deepsearch_read` for architectural understanding
- Follow up with `sg_read_file` on key files mentioned in the response

**When the user is implementing a new feature:**
- Search for similar existing implementations first
- Read tests for usage examples
- Check for shared utilities before creating new ones

**When debugging:**
- Search for the exact error string
- Trace the symbol where the error is thrown
- Check recent changes with `sg_diff_search`

## Workflows

For detailed step-by-step workflows, see:
- `workflows/implementing-feature.md` — when building new features
- `workflows/understanding-code.md` — when exploring unfamiliar systems
- `workflows/debugging-issue.md` — when tracking down bugs

## Efficiency Rules

**Minimise tool calls:**
- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms (faster, more precise)

**Batch your understanding:**
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than reading one and asking questions
- Use `sg_deepsearch_read` for "how does X work" instead of multiple keyword searches

**Avoid common token waste:**
- Don't search all repos when you know the target repo
- Don't use `sg_deepsearch_read` for simple "find all" queries
- Don't re-read files you've already seen in this conversation

## Query Patterns

| Intent | Query |
|--------|-------|
| React hooks | `file:.*\.tsx$ use[A-Z].*= \(` |
| API routes | `file:src/api app\.(get\|post\|put\|delete)` |
| Error handling | `catch.*Error\|\.catch\(` |
| Type definitions | `file:types/ export (interface\|type)` |
| Test setup | `file:.*\.test\. beforeEach\|beforeAll` |
| Config files | `file:(webpack\|vite\|rollup)\.config` |
| CI/CD | `file:\.github/workflows deploy` |

For more patterns, see `query-patterns.md`.

## Output Formatting

**Search results:**
- Present as a brief summary, not raw tool output
- Highlight the most relevant file and line
- Include a code snippet only if it directly answers the question

**Code explanations:**
- Start with a one-sentence summary
- Use the codebase's own terminology
- Reference specific files and functions

**Recommendations:**
- Present as numbered steps if actionable
- Link to specific patterns found in the codebase
- Note any existing utilities that should be reused

## Common Mistakes

| Mistake | Fix |
|---------|-----|
| Searching all repos | Add `repo:^github.com/org/repo$` |
| Too many results | Add `file:` pattern or keywords |
| Missing relevant code | Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching |
| Not understanding context | Use `sg_deepsearch_read` |
| Guessing patterns | Read implementations with `sg_read_file` |

## Principles

- Start narrow, expand if needed
- Chain tools: search → read → find references → definition
- Check tests for usage examples
- Read before generating

# Task: Kubernetes client-go Systems Guide

**Repository:** kubernetes/kubernetes (stripped snapshot)
**Output:** Write your document to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Objective

Produce an advanced systems guide for client-go that explains API access, controller loops, and extension paths.

## Scope

Focus on the client-go subsystem.
Your document must explain component responsibilities, end-to-end flow, and extension or operational tradeoffs.

## Content Expectations

Address all of the following in your own structure:
- subsystem purpose, boundaries, and upstream/downstream dependencies
- key components and how responsibilities are split
- end-to-end control/request flow with concrete repository evidence
- failure modes, limits, and design tradeoffs
- extension points, customization hooks, and associated risks
- a concise map of the most important files/directories used in your analysis

## Quality Bar

- Cite concrete file paths from the repository.
- Explain behavior and interactions, not just API signatures.
- Include at least one section on operational implications (performance, reliability, or maintainability).
- Do not fabricate classes, functions, or files.

## Anti-Requirements

- Do not output `doc.go`.
- Do not provide shallow bullet dumps without system flow explanation.
- Do not rely on external docs not present in the workspace.

88-assistanttext-
The documentation has been written to `/workspace/documentation.md`. Here is a summary of what it covers:

**Structure (7 sections):**

1. **Subsystem Purpose and Boundaries** — where client-go sits between application code and the API server, and what it imports/exports.

2. **Key Components and Responsibility Split** — walks through each layer:
   - `rest.Config` (all client policy in one struct, with exact field citations)
   - The `RoundTripper` chain assembled by `HTTPWrappersForConfig` (auth, debug, user-agent, impersonation)
   - `RESTClient` and the `Request` builder pattern with deferred execution
   - Retry logic via `WithRetry` and 429/5xx handling
   - Typed Clientset, Dynamic Client, and Discovery

3. **End-to-End Control Flow** — concrete walk-through of: a simple API request (token bucket → RoundTripper chain → error deserialization), then the full informer startup sequence (LIST+WATCH bootstrap, DeltaFIFO accumulation, Indexer updates, handler dispatch), ending with the canonical event handler → work queue → reconcile pattern.

4. **Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs** — watch expiry and re-list cost, bookmark events, WatchList mode, slow event handler consequences (ring buffer overflow, `blockDeltas` contention), cache-coherence guarantees, resync semantics, and rate-limiting tradeoffs.

5. **Extension Points and Customization Hooks** — `WrapTransport`, `TransformFunc`, `SetWatchErrorHandlerWithContext`, `WithTweakListOptions`, custom rate limiters, custom Indexers, Server-Side Apply via `applyconfigurations`, and leader election with fencing caveats.

6. **Operational Implications** — memory (one cache per factory instance), CPU (`blockDeltas` mutex contention), API server load (re-list cost and backoff design), correctness (never mutate cache
... (truncated)

It describes the\\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\\n5: \\n6: ## Client Configuration\\n7: \\n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\\n13: \\n14: ## REST Client\\n15: \\n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\\n19: \\n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\\n23: is injected into requests.\\n24: \\n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\\n28: \\n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\\n30: \\n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \\n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\\n46: \\n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\\n48: \\n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\\n57: \\n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\\n59: \\n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\\n61: models.\\n62: \\n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\\n64: \\n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\\n71: \\n72: ## Code Generation\\n73: \\n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\\n77: several key components:\\n78: \\n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\\n83: \\n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\\n86: \\n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\\n88: \\n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\\n90: \\n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\\n93: \\n94: The data flow is as follows:\\n95: \\n96: ```mermaid\\n97: graph TD\\n98:     subgraph \\\"Kubernetes API\\\"\\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\\n100:     end\\n101: \\n102:     subgraph \\\"client-go: Informer Mechanism\\\"\\n103:         Reflector(\\\"1. Reflector\\\")\\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\\\"2. DeltaFIFO\\\")\\n105:         Indexer[\\\"3. Indexer (Cache)\\\"]\\n106:         EventHandlers(\\\"4. Event Handlers\\\")\\n107:     end\\n108: \\n109:     subgraph \\\"User Code\\\"\\n110:         WorkQueue[\\\"5. Work Queue\\\"]\\n111:         Controller(\\\"6. Controller\\\")\\n112:     end\\n113: \\n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\\n121: ```\\n122: \\n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\\n126: **\\\"too old\\\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\\n129: \\n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\\n132: \\n133: ## Controller Patterns\\n134: \\n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\\n136: ensure resiliency.\\n137: \\n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\\n142: \\n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\\n145: shared `Lease` object.\\n146: \\n147: ## Server-Side Apply\\n148: \\n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\\n153: construct the declarative patch.\\n154: \\n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\\n156: \\n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\\n159: \\n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. All are\\n33: // optional.\\n34: type DeltaFIFOOptions struct {\\n35: \\t// If set, log output will go to this logger instead of klog.Background().\\n36: \\t// The name of the fifo gets added automatically.\\n37: \\tLogger *klog.Logger\\n38: \\n39: \\t// Name can be used to override the default \\\"DeltaFIFO\\\" name for the new instance.\\n40: \\tName string\\n41: \\n42: \\t// KeyFunction is used to figure out what key an object should have. (It's\\n43: \\t// exposed in the returned DeltaFIFO's KeyOf() method, with additional\\n44: \\t// handling around deleted objects and queue state).\\n45: \\t// Optional, the default is MetaNamespaceKeyFunc.\\n46: \\tKeyFunction KeyFunc\\n47: \\n48: \\t// KnownObjects is expected to return a list of keys that the consumer of\\n49: \\t// this queue \\\"knows about\\\". It is used to decide which items are missing\\n50: \\t// when Replace() is called; 'Deleted' deltas are produced for the missing items.\\n51: \\t// KnownObjects may be nil if you can tolerate missing deletions on Replace().\\n52: \\tKnownObjects KeyListerGetter\\n53: \\n54: \\t// EmitDeltaTypeReplaced indicates that the queue consumer\\n55: \\t// understands the Replaced DeltaType. Before the `Replaced` event type was\\n56: \\t// added, calls to Replace() were handled the same as Sync(). For\\n57: \\t// backwards-compatibility purposes, this is false by default.\\n58: \\t// When true, `Replaced` events will be sent for items passed to a Replace() call.\\n59: \\t// When false, `Sync` events will be sent instead.\\n60: \\tEmitDeltaTypeReplaced bool\\n61: \\n62: \\t// If set, will be called for objects before enqueueing them. Please\\n63: \\t// see the comment on TransformFunc for details.\\n64: \\tTransformer TransformFunc\\n65: }\\n66: \\n67: // DeltaFIFO is like FIFO, but differs in two ways.  One is that the\\n68: // accumulator associated with a given object's key is not that object\\n69: // but rather a Deltas, which is a slice of Delta values for that\\n70: // object.  Applying an object to a Deltas means to append a Delta\\n71: // except when the potentially appended Delta is a Deleted and the\\n72: // Deltas already ends with a Deleted.  In that case the Deltas does\\n73: // not grow, although the terminal Deleted will be replaced by the new\\n74: // Deleted if the older Deleted's object is a\\n75: // DeletedFinalStateUnknown.\\n76: //\\n77: // The other difference is that DeltaFIFO has two additional ways that\\n78: // an object can be applied to an accumulator: Replaced and Sync.\\n79: // If EmitDeltaTypeReplaced is not set to true, Sync will be used in\\n80: // replace events for backwards compatibility.  Sync is used for periodic\\n81: // resync events.\\n82: //\\n83: // DeltaFIFO is a producer-consumer queue, where a Reflector is\\n84: // intended to be the producer, and the consumer is whatever calls\\n85: // the Pop() method.\\n86: //\\n87: // DeltaFIFO solves this use case:\\n88: //   - You want to process every object change (delta) at most once.\\n89: //   - When you process an object, you want to see everything\\n90: //     that's happened to it since you last processed it.\\n91: //   - You want to process the deletion of some of the objects.\\n92: //   - You might want to periodically reprocess objects.\\n93: //\\n94: // DeltaFIFO's Pop(), Get(), and GetByKey() methods return\\n95: // interface{} to satisfy the Store/Queue interfaces, but they\\n96: // will always return an object of type Deltas. List() returns\\n97: // the newest object from each accumulator in the FIFO.\\n98: //\\n99: // A DeltaFIFO's knownObjects KeyListerGetter provides the abilities\\n100: // to list Store keys and to get objects by Store key.  The objects in\\n101: // question are called \\\"known objects\\\" and this set of objects\\n102: // modifies the behavior of the Delete, Replace, and Resync methods\\n103: // (each in a different way).\\n104: //\\n105: // A note on threading: If you call Pop() in parallel from multiple\\n106: // threads, you could end up with multiple threads processing slightly\\n107: // different versions of the same object.\\n108: type DeltaFIFO struct {\\n109: \\t// logger is a per-instance logger. This gets chosen when constructing\\n110: \\t// the instance, with klog.Background() as default.\\n111: \\tlogger klog.Logger\\n112: \\n113: \\t// name is the name of the fifo. It is included in the logger.\\n114: \\tname string\\n115: \\n116: \\t// lock/cond protects access to 'items' and 'queue'.\\n117: \\tlock sync.RWMutex\\n118: \\tcond sync.Cond\\n119: \\n120: \\t// `items` maps a key to a Deltas.\\n121: \\t// Each such Deltas has at least one Delta.\\n122: \\titems map[string]Deltas\\n123: \\n124: \\t// `queue` maintains FIFO order of keys for consumption in Pop().\\n125: \\t// There are no duplicates in `queue`.\\n126: \\t// A key is in `queue` if and only if it is in `items`.\\n127: \\tqueue []string\\n128: \\n129: \\t// synced is initially an open channel. This implementation does not guarantee that only one\\n20: // client is acting as a leader (a.k.a. fencing).\\n21: //\\n22: // A client only acts on timestamps captured locally to infer the state of the\\n23: // leader election. The client does not consider timestamps in the leader\\n24: // election record to be accurate because these timestamps may not have been\\n25: // produced by a local clock. The implemention does not depend on their\\n26: // accuracy and only uses their change to indicate that another client has\\n27: // renewed the leader lease. Thus the implementation is tolerant to arbitrary\\n28: // clock skew, but is not tolerant to arbitrary clock skew rate.\\n29: //\\n30: // However the level of tolerance to skew rate can be configured by setting\\n31: // RenewDeadline and LeaseDuration appropriately. The tolerance expressed as a\\n32: // maximum tolerated ratio of time passed on the fastest node to time passed on\\n33: // the slowest node can be approximately achieved with a configuration that sets\\n34: // the same ratio of LeaseDuration to RenewDeadline. For example if a user wanted\\n35: // to tolerate some nodes progressing forward in time twice as fast as other nodes,\\n36: // the user could set LeaseDuration to 60 seconds and RenewDeadline to 30 seconds.\\n37: //\\n38: // While not required, some method of clock synchronization between nodes in the\\n39: // cluster is highly recommended. It's important to keep in mind when configuring\\n40: // this client that the tolerance to skew rate varies inversely to master\\n41: // availability.\\n42: //\\n43: // Larger clusters often have a more lenient SLA for API latency. This should be\\n44: // taken into account when configuring the client. The rate of leader transitions\\n45: // should be monitored and RetryPeriod and LeaseDuration should be increased\\n46: // until the rate is stable and acceptably low.   "structuredContent": {
    "content": "1: /*\n2: Copyright 2015 The Kubernetes Authors.\n3: \n4: Licensed under the Apache License, Version 2.0 (the \"License\");\n5: you may not use this file except in compliance with the License.\n6: You may obtain a copy of the License at\n7: \n8:     http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\n9: \n10: Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\n11: distributed under the License is distributed on an \"AS IS\" BASIS,\n12: WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\n13: See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\n14: limitations under the License.\n15: */\n16: \n17: // Package leaderelection implements leader election of a set of endpoints.\n18: // It uses an annotation in the endpoints object to store the record of the\n19: // election state. This implementation does not guarantee that only one\n20: // client is acting as a leader (a.k.a. fencing).\n21: //\n22: // A client only acts on timestamps captured locally to infer the state of the\n23: // leader election. The client does not consider timestamps in the leader\n24: // election record to be accurate because these timestamps may not have been\n25: // produced by a local clock. The implemention does not depend on their\n26: // accuracy and only uses their change to indicate that another client has\n27: // renewed the leader lease. Thus the implementation is tolerant to arbitrary\n28: // clock skew, but is not tolerant to arbitrary clock skew rate.\n29: //\n30: // However the level of tolerance to skew rate can be configured by setting\n31: // RenewDeadline and LeaseDuration appropriately. The tolerance expressed as a\n32: // maximum tolerated ratio of time passed on the fastest node to time passed on\n33: // the slowest node can be approximately achieved with a configuration that sets\n34: // the same ratio of LeaseDuration to RenewDeadline. For example if a user wanted\n35: // to tolerate some nodes progressing forward in time twice as fast as other nodes,\n36: // the user could set LeaseDuration to 60 seconds and RenewDeadline to 30 seconds.\n37: //\n38: // While not required, some method of clock synchronization between nodes in the\n39: // cluster is highly recommended. It's important to keep in mind when configuring\n40: // this client that the tolerance to skew rate varies inversely to master\n41: // availability.\n42: //\n43: // Larger clusters often have a more lenient SLA for API latency. This should be\n44: // taken into account when configuring the client. The rate of leader transitions\n45: // should be monitored and RetryPeriod and LeaseDuration should be increased\n46: // until the rate is stable and acceptably low. It's important to keep in mind\n47: // when configuring this client that the tolerance to API latency varies inversely\n48: // to master availability.\n49: //\n50: // DISCLAIMER: this is an alpha API. A\n29: // Store holds a map from string keys to accumulators, and has\n30: // operations to add, update, and delete a given object to/from the\n31: // accumulator currently associated with a given key.  A Store also\n32: // knows how to extract the key from a given object, so many operations\n33: // are given only the object.\n34: //\n35: // In the simplest Store implementations each accumulator is simply\n36: // the last given object, or empty after Delete, and thus the Store's\n37: // behavior is simple storage.\n38: //\n39: // Reflector knows how to watch a server and update a Store.  This\n40: // package provides a variety of implementations of Store.\n41: type Store interface {\n42: \n43: \t// Add adds the given object to the accumulator associated with the given object's key\n44: \tAdd(obj interface{}) error\n45: \n46: \t// Update updates the given object in the accumulator associated with the given object's key\n47: \tUpdate(obj interface{}) error\n48: \n49: \t// Delete deletes the given object from the accumulator associated with the given object's key\n50: \tDelete(obj interface{}) error\n51: \n52: \t// List returns a list of all the currently non-empty accumulators\n53: \tList() []interface{}\n54: \n55: \t// ListKeys returns a list of all the keys currently associated with non-empty accumulators\n56: \tListKeys() []string\n57: \n58: \t// LastStoreSyncResourceVersion returns the latest resource version that the store has seen.\n59: \t// This is used to determine the latest resource version the store has seen from objects\n60: \t// observed being written to the store.\n61: \tLastStoreSyncResourceVersion() string\n62: \n63: \t// Bookmark observes a new resource version passed into it and\n64: \t// will be used to get the latest resource version of the store.\n65: \tBookmark(rv string)\n66: \n67: \t// Get returns the accumulator associated with the given object's key\n68: \tGet(obj interface{}) (item interface{}, exists bool, err error)\n69: \n70: \t// GetByKey returns the accumulator associated with the given key\n71: \tGetByKey(key string) (item interface{}, exists bool, err error)\n72: \n73: \t// Replace will delete the contents of the store, using instead the\n74: \t// given list. Store takes ownership of the list, you should not reference\n75: \t// it after calling this function.\n76: \tReplace([]interface{}, string) error\n77: \n78: \t// Resync is meaningless in the terms appearing here but has\n79: \t// meaning in some implementations that have non-trivial\n80: \t// additional behavior (e.g., DeltaFIFO).\n81: \tResync() error\n82: }\n83: \n84: // TransactionType defines the type of a transaction operation. It is used to indicate whether\n85: // an object is being added, updated, or deleted.\n86: type TransactionType string\n87: \n88: const (\n89: \tTransactionTypeAdd    TransactionType = \"Add\"\n90: \tTransactionTypeUpdate TransactionType = \"Update\"\n91: \tTransactionTypeDelete TransactionType = \"Delete\"\n92: )\n93: \n94: // Transaction represents a single operation or event in a process. It holds a generic Object\n95: // associated with the transaction and a Type indicating the kind of transaction being performed.\n96: type Transaction struct {\n97: \tObject interface{}\n98: \tType   TransactionType\n99: }\n100: \n101: ",

{
  "content": "# client-go Advanced Systems Guide: API Access, Controller Loops, and Extension Paths\n\n## 1. Subsystem Purpose and Boundaries\n\n`client-go` is the canonical Go client library for the Kubernetes API. It sits between application code (controllers, operators, CLI tools) and the Kubernetes API server. Its responsibilities span three distinct tiers:\n\n1. **Wire-level communication**: building and executing HTTP requests, negotiating serialization formats, handling authentication, and managing retries and rate limiting.\n2. **Cache-and-watch infrastructure**: maintaining an eventually-consistent local mirror of API server state, so controllers can read objects without hammering the API server on every reconcile.\n3. **Controller primitives**: work queues, leader election, and event recording \u2014 the glue that turns a stream of cache events into a reliable, rate-limited reconciliation loop.\n\n**Upstream dependency**: `client-go` imports `k8s.io/apimachinery` for types, scheme registration, and error helpers, and `k8s.io/api` for concrete Go structs for built-in API groups. It does not import from the main `k8s.io/kubernetes` module; the code lives under `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/` and is published separately.\n\n**Downstream consumers**: every controller in `k8s.io/kubernetes` (kube-controller-manager, scheduler, kubelet, and so on) consumes `client-go`. External operators and CRD controllers generated by `k8s.io/code-generator` consume it too.\n\n---\n\n## 2. Key Components and Responsibility Split\n\n### 2.1 Configuration Layer: `rest.Config`\n\n`rest.Config` (`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/config.go`) is the single struct that represents all client-level policy:\n\n- **Endpoint**: `Host`, `APIPath`\n- **Auth**: `BearerToken`, `BearerTokenFile`, `Username`/`Password`, `ExecProvider` (credential plugin), `AuthProvider` (legacy OIDC)\n- **TLS**: `TLSClientConfig` \u2014 inline cert/key PEM data, CA file, server name override\n- **Transport customization**: `Transport` (override entirely) or `WrapTransport` (layer additional `RoundTripper`s)\n- **Rate limits**: `QPS float32`, `Burst int`, `RateLimiter` \u2014 the client-side token bucket\n\nDefaults are `DefaultQPS = 5.0` and `DefaultBurst = 10` (config.go:47-48). Setting `QPS` to a negative value disables client-side rate limiting (config.go:120-121).\n\n`rest.Config` is not used directly by application code to make calls; it is used by factory functions (`kubernetes.NewForConfig`, `dynamic.NewForConfig`, etc.) to construct specialized client objects.\n\n### 2.2 Transport Layer: RoundTripper Chain\n\n`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/transport/round_trippers.go` assembles authentication and debugging middleware. `HTTPWrappersForConfig` (round_trippers.go:41) layers `RoundTripper`s in this order outward-in:\n\n1. `config.WrapTransport` \u2014 user-supplied middleware (runs first)\n2. `DebugWrappers` \u2014 request/response logging when `klog.V(6)` is enabled\n3. Authentication: `BearerAuthWithRefreshRoundTripper` (token or token file), `BasicAuthRoundTripper`, or nothing\n4. `UserAgentRoundTripper`\n5. `ImpersonatingRoundTripper` \u2014 sets `Impersonate-User` and related headers\n\nEach layer is an `http.RoundTripper` that wraps the next. This chain is constructed once at startup from `rest.Config` and is shared across all requests from a given client instance.\n\n`BearerTokenFile` support means credentials can be rotated on disk (as is standard with projected service account tokens); the `cert_rotation.go` file in the `transport/` package handles certificate rotation similarly.\n\n### 2.3 REST Client: `rest.RESTClient` and `rest.Request`\n\n`rest.RESTClient` (`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/client.go`) holds the `http.Client`, the rate limiter, the codec factory, and the base URL. It exposes verb methods (`.Get()`, `.Post()`, `.Put()`, `.Delete()`, `.Patch()`) that return a `*rest.Request`.\n\n`rest.Request` (`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/request.go`) implements a **builder pattern**: callers chain methods like `.Namespace()`, `.Resource()`, `.Name()`, `.Body()`, `.Param()` to assemble the URL and request body. Execution is deferred until terminal methods are called:\n- `.Do(ctx)` \u2014 executes and deserializes to a typed object\n- `.DoRaw(ctx)` \u2014 executes and returns raw bytes\n- `.Watch(ctx)` \u2014 opens a streaming watch\n- `.Stream(ctx)` \u2014 streams arbitrary response bodies (e.g., pod logs)\n\nThe `Request` struct carries a `rateLimiter` (`flowcontrol.RateLimiter`) and a `BackoffManagerWithContext`. Before each attempt, the rate limiter is consulted; if throttled for more than `longThrottleLatency` (50ms), the delay is logged (request.go:54-57).\n\n### 2.4 Retry Logic\n\n`rest/with_retry.go` defines the `WithRetry` interface. The default `withRetry` implementation (`defaultRequestRetryFn`) retries on server-side 429 and 5xx responses by reading the `Retry-After` response header. Callers can inject a custom `IsRetryableErrorFunc` to extend retry decisions. Watch and stream calls use a separate, non-retrying path by design \u2014 the reconnect loop is handled at the reflector level.\n\n### 2.5 Typed Clientset: `kubernetes.Clientset`\n\n`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/kubernetes/` is generated code providing a type-safe client for every built-in API group. Each group (e.g., `AppsV1()`, `CoreV1()`) returns a typed interface exposing `Get`, `List`, `Watch`, `Create`, `Update`, `Patch`, `Delete`, and subresource operations. These functions internally call through to a `rest.RESTClient`.\n\nThe interface hierarchy is: `kubernetes.Interface` \u2192 group interface (e.g., `AppsV1Interface`) \u2192 resource interface (e.g., `DeploymentInterface`).\n\n### 2.6 Dynamic Client: `dynamic.DynamicClient`\n\n`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/dynamic/` provides a client that represents every object as `*unstructured.Unstructured`. This is the standard path for CRD controllers and tools that must work with unknown types. It requires no code generation but also provides no compile-time type safety.\n\n### 2.7 Discovery: `discovery.DiscoveryClient` and Caching\n\nThe `discovery` package provides `DiscoveryClient` for querying `/api` and `/apis` to enumerate available API groups and resources. `CachedDiscoveryClient` adds a disk cache (`~/.kube/cache/discovery/`) to avoid re-fetching the API surface on every startup. The `restmapper` package builds a `RESTMapper` from discovery data, translating `GroupVersionKind` \u2194 `GroupVersionResource`, which is required by the dynamic client to construct correct URLs.\n\n---\n\n## 3. End-to-End Control Flow\n\n### 3.1 Simple API Request\n\n1. Application calls e.g. `clientset.CoreV1().Pods(\"default\").Get(ctx, \"mypod\", metav1.GetOptions{})`.\n2. The generated typed client calls `c.client.Get()` on its underlying `*rest.RESTClient`, which constructs a `*rest.Request` for `GET /api/v1/namespaces/default/pods/mypod`.\n3. Before the HTTP call, `Request.tryThrottleWithContext` consults the `flowcontrol.RateLimiter` \u2014 it blocks until the token bucket has capacity.\n4. The backoff manager (`urlbackoff.go`) may add a delay if the URL's server has recently returned errors.\n5. The `http.Client` executes the request through the `RoundTripper` chain, injecting auth headers, logging if enabled.\n6. The response is deserialized: a 2xx body is decoded via the codec factory into the requested Go type; a non-2xx body is decoded into `metav1.Status` and wrapped in `apierrors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic checks like `apierrors.IsNotFound(err)`.\n\n### 3.2 Informer Startup: the LIST+WATCH Bootstrap\n\nThe heart of the controller pattern is the `sharedIndexInformer` (shared_informer.go:587), which composes three sub-components:\n\n```\nsharedIndexInformer\n  \u251c\u2500\u2500 controller (tools/cache/controller.go)\n  \u2502     \u251c\u2500\u2500 Reflector      \u2014 performs LIST+WATCH against API server\n  \u2502     \u2514\u2500\u2500 DeltaFIFO      \u2014 accumulates object deltas\n  \u251c\u2500\u2500 Indexer               \u2014 in-memory thread-safe map with secondary indices\n  \u2514\u2500\u2500 sharedProcessor       \u2014 dispatches events to registered event handlers\n```\n\nWhen `sharedIndexInformer.RunWithContext(ctx)` is called (shared_informer.go:718):\n1. A `DeltaFIFO` is created with the `Indexer` as its `knownObjects` source.\n2. A `controller.Config` is assembled, binding the `DeltaFIFO`, the `ListerWatcher`, and a `Process` function that calls `handleDeltas`.\n3. The `controller` is started; it launches the `Reflector`.\n4. Concurrently, `sharedProcessor.run` starts goroutines for each registered event handler listener.\n\n### 3.3 Reflector: LIST+WATCH Loop\n\n`Reflector.RunWithContext` (reflector.go:423) drives the outer retry loop using `wait.DelayFunc.Until`. It calls `ListAndWatchWithContext` repeatedly, backing off exponentially on errors (base 800ms, max 30s \u2014 reflector.go:62-66).\n\n`ListAndWatchWithContext` (reflector.go:472):\n1. **WatchList mode** (if `useWatchList` is set, feature gate `WatchListClient`): opens a streaming watch to receive the initial list, falling back to paginated LIST on failure.\n2. **Standard LIST**: calls the `ListerWatcher.List` implementation through the `tools/pager` package, which automatically handles server-side pagination by following `continue` tokens.\n3. Calls `store.Replace(items, resourceVersion)` to atomically populate the `DeltaFIFO` with all current objects.\n4. Calls `watchWithResync` which opens a WATCH at the observed `resourceVersion`, then processes `watch.Event` objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`) from the stream, calling `store.Add`, `store.Update`, `store.Delete` on the `DeltaFIFO`.\n5. On `BOOKMARK` events, the `resourceVersion` in `lastSyncResourceVersion` is updated; this is the starting point for the next reconnect if the watch dies.\n\n**Resync** is handled in a separate goroutine (`startResync`, reflector.go:516): on a timer tick, it calls `store.Resync()` on the `DeltaFIFO`, which re-enqueues all known objects as `Sync` deltas. This causes the controller to re-run its reconcile logic for every object even if nothing changed.\n\n### 3.4 DeltaFIFO: The Change Accumulator\n\n`DeltaFIFO` (delta_fifo.go:108) is a keyed producer-consumer queue. Each key maps to a `Deltas` slice \u2014 an ordered list of `Delta{Type, Object}` entries where `Type` is one of `Added`, `Updated`, `Deleted`, `Replaced`, or `Sync`.\n\nThe critical design property: if the same key is modified multiple times while the consumer is busy processing a previous entry, the new modifications are **appended** to that key's `Deltas` slice. The queue holds the key once; successive events accumulate. When `Pop()` is finally called, the consumer sees the full ordered history since the last pop. This means the controller always processes the **most recent known state** even if several API events arrived in quick succession.\n\n`DeltaFIFO` also deduplicates consecutive `Deleted` events: if the terminal delta for a key is already `Deleted`, a new `Deleted` is collapsed into it (replacing the old one if it was a `DeletedFinalStateUnknown`). This matters when the watch reconnects: the reflector calls `Replace`, and any object not in the new list gets a synthetic `Deleted` delta.\n\n### 3.5 From DeltaFIFO to Indexer and Event Handlers\n\nThe `controller` pops `Deltas` from the `DeltaFIFO` and calls the `Config.Process` function, which is `sharedIndexInformer.handleDeltas` (shared_informer.go:940). Under `blockDeltas` mutex:\n1. For each `Delta`, the `Indexer` is updated (add/update/delete in the local cache).\n2. The `sharedIndexInformer` itself implements `ResourceEventHandler`, calling `processor.distribute(notification, isSync)` to fan out to all registered listeners.\n\n`sharedProcessor.distribute` (shared_informer.go) pushes the notification into each `processorListener`'s channel. Each listener runs in its own goroutine, draining the channel and calling the user's `OnAdd`/`OnUpdate`/`OnDelete` callback.\n\n### 3.6 Event Handler to Work Queue to Reconcile\n\nThe canonical pattern:\n\n```go\ninformer.AddEventHandler(cache.ResourceEventHandlerFuncs{\n    AddFunc:    func(obj interface{}) { queue.Add(key(obj)) },\n    UpdateFunc: func(old, new interface{}) { queue.Add(key(new)) },\n    DeleteFunc: func(obj interface{}) { queue.Add(key(obj)) },\n})\n// ... in worker goroutines:\nfor {\n    key, quit := queue.Get()\n    if quit { return }\n    err := reconcile(key)\n    if err != nil {\n        queue.AddRateLimited(key)\n    } else {\n        queue.Forget(key)\n    }\n    queue.Done(key)\n}\n```\n\nThe `workqueue.TypedRateLimitingInterface[T]` (`util/workqueue/rate_limiting_queue.go:27`) composes:\n- A base `TypedInterface[T]` (deduplicating FIFO)\n- A `TypedDelayingInterface[T]` (adds items after a delay, used for rate limiting)\n- A `TypedRateLimiter[T]` \u2014 the `DefaultTypedControllerRateLimiter` uses `TypedItemExponentialFailureRateLimiter` (5ms base, 1000s max) combined with a token bucket (10 QPS, burst 100) via `TypedMaxOfRateLimiter` (default_rate_limiters.go:50-56)\n\nThe **key insight** of this design: event handlers are fast (only enqueue a string key) and never block the watch stream. The actual work, including API calls and potentially slow business logic, happens in the worker goroutines behind the queue. If a reconcile fails, `AddRateLimited` re-enqueues the key with exponential backoff, and `Forget` clears the failure count on success.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs\n\n### 4.1 Watch Expiry and \"Too Old resourceVersion\"\n\nIf a watch connection is idle for long enough, or if the API server's watch cache is too small, the server returns a `410 Gone` with a `StatusReasonExpired` error. The `DefaultWatchErrorHandler` (reflector.go:215) logs this at V(4) and returns without marking `isLastSyncResourceVersionUnavailable`. The reflector's outer loop re-calls `ListAndWatch`, which re-lists from the server, re-populates the cache, and starts a new watch. This re-list is the fundamental recovery mechanism: it is correct but expensive under high churn.\n\n`lastSyncResourceVersionMutex` (reflector.go:147) guards access to `lastSyncResourceVersion` and `isLastSyncResourceVersionUnavailable` to avoid races between the watch loop and resync timer.\n\n### 4.2 Bookmark Events\n\n`BOOKMARK` events (`watch.EventType = \"BOOKMARK\"`) carry an updated `resourceVersion` but no object data. The reflector stores them in `lastSyncResourceVersion` (via the optional `ReflectorBookmarkStore` interface) so that reconnects restart from a more recent position, reducing the cost of recovery after a transient network failure.\n\n### 4.3 WatchList (Streaming Initialization)\n\nThe `useWatchList` flag (reflector.go:170) enables KEP-3157: instead of a paginated LIST followed by a WATCH, the initial sync uses a single streaming WATCH request that terminates after the server signals the list is complete (via a `BOOKMARK` with `initialEventsEndBookmark` annotation). This reduces memory pressure on the API server for large collections because the server need not assemble a complete list snapshot. The reflector falls back to the traditional LIST path on error (reflector.go:492-498).\n\n### 4.4 Slow Event Handlers\n\nThe `sharedIndexInformer` comment (shared_informer.go:134-138) is explicit: \"A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of notifications to deliver.\" Each `processorListener` has a ring buffer (`utils/buffer`) of initial size 1024 (`initialBufferSize`, shared_informer.go:371). If the handler is slow, this buffer fills up, and the listener's goroutine blocks distributing notifications, which in turn holds the `blockDeltas` lock and stalls the entire informer's processing pipeline.\n\n**The prescribed fix**: event handlers must be fast. Any non-trivial work belongs in a worker goroutine consuming a work queue, with the event handler only enqueuing a string key.\n\n### 4.5 Cache-Coherence Guarantee\n\nThe `Indexer` provides a thread-safe read path. Reads from inside a reconcile loop should always target the `Lister`/`Indexer`, never the API server, for two reasons:\n1. **Cost**: API server reads are expensive; Indexer reads are memory operations.\n2. **Consistency**: the informer guarantees that by the time an `OnUpdate` handler fires, the `Indexer` reflects the new state. Reading from the `Indexer` inside a reconcile therefore always sees a state at least as new as the triggering event.\n\nHowever, the Indexer's state is eventually consistent, not linearizable. A controller that writes to the API server and then immediately reads back may see a stale version via the Indexer if the watch has not yet propagated the write. The `MutationCache` (`tools/cache/mutation_cache.go:34`) provides a short-term LRU overlay that merges recent write results with Indexer reads as a mitigation.\n\n### 4.6 Resync vs. Real Events\n\nResync events (`isSync = true` in `OnUpdate`) do not represent API server changes; they are synthetic re-deliveries of the current cache state triggered on a timer to ensure controllers eventually reconcile even if an event was missed. Controllers must be written to be **idempotent**: applying the same desired state twice must have no harmful effect.\n\n### 4.7 Client-Side Rate Limiting Tradeoff\n\nThe QPS/Burst defaults (`DefaultQPS = 5.0`, `DefaultBurst = 10`) are conservative. Operators running many controllers or watching large namespaces frequently need higher values. However, raising them blindly without regard for API server capacity (APF priority/fairness) can cause cascading load problems. The recommended approach is to tune these values based on measured API server latency under load, not to simply set them to very high numbers.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Extension Points and Customization Hooks\n\n### 5.1 `WrapTransport` \u2014 Custom RoundTripper Middleware\n\n`rest.Config.WrapTransport` accepts a `transport.WrapperFunc` that wraps the final `http.RoundTripper`. This is the correct place to inject:\n- Custom auth token sources (e.g., for non-standard identity providers)\n- Request/response logging or metrics exporters\n- Mutual TLS or proxy injection\n\n**Risk**: each `rest.Config` instance must have its own `WrapTransport` set before creating clients. Once the `http.Client` is built, the transport chain is immutable. Modifications after construction are silently ignored.\n\n### 5.2 `TransformFunc` \u2014 In-Informer Object Trimming\n\n`SharedInformer.SetTransform(handler TransformFunc)` (shared_informer.go:243) installs a function that runs on every object before it is stored in the `Indexer`. This is the correct place to strip unused fields (e.g., managed fields, large annotations) to reduce in-memory footprint.\n\n**Constraint**: `SetTransform` must be called before `Run`; calling it afterward returns an error (shared_informer.go:706). The transform must not modify the original object if the object may be shared; it should return a new object or a shallow copy.\n\n### 5.3 `SetWatchErrorHandlerWithContext` \u2014 Watch Error Visibility\n\nInformers expose a single `WatchErrorHandlerWithContext` hook for observing watch disconnections. This is suitable for metrics emission or surfacing errors to a health endpoint.\n\n**Constraint**: it is for **visibility only** \u2014 the handler must return quickly and must not attempt to pause or restart the informer. The handler is set once; subsequent calls replace the previous handler (shared_informer.go:220-221).\n\n### 5.4 `WithTweakListOptions` \u2014 Informer Filtering\n\n`SharedInformerFactory` (informers/factory.go) accepts `WithTweakListOptions(func(*metav1.ListOptions))`, allowing injection of label selectors and field selectors into every list/watch call issued by informers built from that factory. This is how namespace-scoped controllers (e.g., restricting to a single namespace) are correctly implemented at the factory level rather than post-filtering in event handlers.\n\n**Risk**: field selectors passed here are evaluated at the API server on every LIST. Relying on field selectors that are not indexed in etcd will cause full scans and high server-side load.\n\n### 5.5 Work Queue Rate Limiters\n\n`DefaultTypedControllerRateLimiter` (default_rate_limiters.go:50) is the standard choice but is not always appropriate. Controllers with low-latency requirements can substitute an `ItemBucketRateLimiter` with a higher per-item rate. Controllers managing very large numbers of objects may need to tune `maxDelay` downward to avoid long tail retries piling up.\n\nThe `TypedRateLimiter[T]` interface (`When`, `Forget`, `NumRequeues`) is simple to implement for custom strategies.\n\n### 5.6 Custom Indexers\n\n`SharedIndexInformer.AddIndexers(Indexers)` installs secondary indices on the in-memory store. An `IndexFunc` maps an object to zero or more string values. Controllers that frequently look up objects by fields other than namespace/name (e.g., \"all Pods for this Node\") benefit significantly from adding a custom `IndexFunc` rather than calling `.List()` and filtering.\n\n**Constraint**: `AddIndexers` must be called before the informer starts (`stopped` must be false, shared_informer.go:836-844).\n\n### 5.7 Server-Side Apply via `applyconfigurations`\n\nThe `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/applyconfigurations/` tree contains generated types for building `Apply` patches. Rather than the traditional GET-modify-UPDATE (which requires tracking the full object and is prone to conflict), the SSA model allows a controller to declare only the fields it owns. The generated types (e.g., `corev1ac.Pod()`) provide a fluent builder API. The call ultimately goes through `client.CoreV1().Pods(ns).Apply(ctx, config, metav1.ApplyOptions{FieldManager: \"my-controller\"})`.\n\n**Risk**: mixing SSA and regular Update on the same fields creates conflicts (409 Conflict). Controllers must choose one model per set of fields and use consistent `FieldManager` names across restarts.\n\n### 5.8 Leader Election\n\n`tools/leaderelection/` provides the `LeaderElector` type. Configuration requires:\n- `Lock rl.Interface` \u2014 backed by a `LeaseLock` (leaselock.go), which reads/writes a `coordination.k8s.io/v1.Lease` object\n- `LeaseDuration` (default 15s), `RenewDeadline` (default 10s), `RetryPeriod` (default 2s)\n- Callbacks: `OnStartedLeading(ctx context.Context)`, `OnStoppedLeading()`, `OnNewLeader(identity string)`\n\nThe lock protocol is **optimistic**: only the current holder renews. A non-holder waits `LeaseDuration` without observing a renewal before it attempts to take over. The implementation explicitly notes (leaderelection.go:27-28): \"This implementation does not guarantee that only one client is acting as a leader (a.k.a. fencing).\" Under extreme clock skew or GC pauses, two processes may briefly both believe they hold the lease.\n\n**Operational implication**: `ReleaseOnCancel: true` in `LeaderElectionConfig` (leaderelection.go:153) allows graceful stepdown when the context is canceled, reducing unnecessary `LeaseDuration` wait for the next leader. It requires that all actions guarded by the lease complete before cancellation.\n\nThe `HealthzAdaptor` (leaderelection/healthzadaptor.go) integrates with `/healthz` so that a liveness probe can fail if the process has been unable to renew the lease within the deadline, allowing Kubernetes to restart it.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Operational Implications\n\n### 6.1 Memory: One Informer Per Resource Type Per Factory\n\n`sharedInformerFactory` (informers/factory.go:69) stores one `SharedIndexInformer` per `reflect.Type`. If two parts of a controller manager both call `factory.CoreV1().Pods(\"\").Informer()`, they receive the **same instance**. This is the \"shared\" in the name \u2014 the watch connection and in-memory cache are amortized across all consumers.\n\nThe flip side: each distinct `SharedInformerFactory` (e.g., one per namespace) holds its own complete copy of every watched resource. In clusters with many namespaces and large numbers of objects, this can be a significant source of memory pressure. A common mitigation is using a single cluster-scoped factory and performing namespace filtering in the event handlers or via `WithTweakListOptions`.\n\n### 6.2 CPU: `blockDeltas` Mutex Contention\n\n`handleDeltas` (shared_informer.go:940) and `AddEventHandlerWithOptions` (shared_informer.go:876) both hold `blockDeltas`. High event rates for a given informer combined with slow handler dispatching can cause the `blockDeltas` mutex to become a bottleneck. Monitoring the reflector's event processing latency (via `reflector_metrics.go`) is the diagnostic signal.\n\n### 6.3 API Server Load: ListAndWatch Re-list Cost\n\nEach Reflector re-list issues a full `LIST` against etcd (unless the watch cache can serve it). In the default paginated mode, this is broken into chunks of `pager.PageSize`. For resources with many objects (e.g., Pods cluster-wide), a re-list is expensive. Frequent re-lists (caused by watch expiry, network instability, or frequent Reflector restarts) significantly increase etcd read load. The reflector's backoff (`defaultBackoffInit = 800ms`, `defaultBackoffMax = 30s`, ~0.22 QPS) is designed to protect the API server under failure conditions.\n\n### 6.4 Correctness: Never Mutate Cached Objects\n\nThe `MutationDetector` (`tools/cache/mutation_detector.go`) is an optional development-time check that panics if a cached object is mutated after being stored. In production, `cacheMutationDetector` is a no-op. Controllers must not mutate objects received in event handler callbacks or returned from Lister calls without first deep-copying them (`obj.DeepCopy()`), because the underlying Indexer may be serving the same pointer to other goroutines.\n\n### 6.5 Feature Gates: `clientgofeaturegate`\n\n`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/features/` manages client-go's own feature gates, separate from the API server's feature gates. Relevant gates include `WatchListClient` (enables WatchList mode in the Reflector) and `InOrderInformersBatchProcess` (enables batch processing in the sharedIndexInformer via `ProcessBatch`). These gates are read at runtime, allowing gradual rollout without recompilation. Client-go feature gates must not be confused with server-side feature gates; they control only client behavior.\n\n---\n\n## 7. File and Directory Map\n\n| Path | Role |\n|------|------|\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md` | Canonical architectural overview maintained by the project |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/config.go` | `rest.Config` struct \u2014 all client-level policy in one place |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/client.go` | `RESTClient` \u2014 the base HTTP client |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/request.go` | `Request` builder; rate limiting, retry, execution paths |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/with_retry.go` | `WithRetry` interface; 429/5xx retry logic |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/transport/round_trippers.go` | Auth/debug `RoundTripper` middleware chain |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/transport/cert_rotation.go` | Certificate auto-rotation for long-lived clients |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/kubernetes/` | Generated typed clientset for all built-in API groups |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/dynamic/` | Unstructured dynamic client for CRDs and unknown types |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/discovery/` | API group/resource enumeration |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/informers/factory.go` | `SharedInformerFactory` \u2014 lifecycle and deduplication of informers |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/shared_informer.go` | `SharedIndexInformer` / `sharedIndexInformer` implementation |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/reflector.go` | `Reflector` \u2014 LIST+WATCH loop, bookmark tracking, backoff |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/delta_fifo.go` | `DeltaFIFO` \u2014 change accumulation queue |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/store.go` | `Store` and `Indexer` interfaces |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/index.go` | `Indexer` interface; `IndexFunc`, `Indexers`, `Indices` types |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/listwatch.go` | `ListerWatcher` interface; context-aware wrappers |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/controller.go` | Low-level `controller` \u2014 drives Reflector \u2192 DeltaFIFO \u2192 Process |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/mutation_cache.go` | `MutationCache` \u2014 LRU overlay for post-write read-back freshness |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/queue.go` | `TypedInterface` \u2014 deduplicating FIFO work queue |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/rate_limiting_queue.go` | `TypedRateLimitingInterface` \u2014 retry queue with backoff |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/default_rate_limiters.go` | `DefaultTypedControllerRateLimiter` \u2014 exponential + bucket |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/leaderelection.go` | `LeaderElector` \u2014 acquire/renew/release loop |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/resourcelock/interface.go` | `resourcelock.Interface` \u2014 abstract lock backend |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/resourcelock/leaselock.go` | `LeaseLock` \u2014 Lease-based implementation (current default) |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/applyconfigurations/` | Generated SSA builder types for all built-in API objects |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/features/` | Client-go feature gates (e.g., `WatchListClient`) |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/flowcontrol/` | Token-bucket rate limiter used by `rest.Config.RateLimiter` |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/clientcmd/` | kubeconfig parsing, context merging, auth provider loading |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/pager/` | Automatic LIST pagination using `continue` tokens |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/watchlist/` | WatchList utilities for streaming initial-list semantics |\n",
  "file_path": "/workspace/documentation.md"
